The amount of writing on Nietzsche in English alone is now growing at a rate that is both a tribute and a threat. The most 
magisterial book on him, by someone deeply sympathetic yet firmly critical, is Erich Hellerโs [3mThe Importance of Nietzsche [0m
(University of Chicago Press, Chicago, 1988). A book somewhat similar in tone, but following patiently through Nietzscheโs 
development, is F. A. Leaโs [3mThe Tragic Philosopher [0m(Athlone Press, London, 1993). Originally published in 1957, it is a 
trailblazing work, written, like Hellerโs and unlike almost everyone elseโs, with notable grace and a Nietzschean passion. 
Unfortunately Lea uses old and discredited translations for quotation; and he ends surprisingly by finding that Nietzsche 
rediscovered the teachings of Christ and Paul for our time. Walter Kaufmannโs ill-organized transformation of Nietzsche into a 
liberal humanist has its place in the history of Nietzsche reception ( [3mNietzsche [0m4th edn, Princeton University Press, Princeton, 
NJ, 1974). 

Of more recent works, the most acclaimed, often setting new standards in detailed analytic working-through of Nietzscheโs 
positions, is Alexander Nehamasโs [3mNietzsche: Life as Literature [0m(Harvard University Press, Cambridge, Mass., 1985). It is a 
demanding but rewarding book, but Nehamas relies too heavily on unpublished notebooks of Nietzscheโs. More impressive still, as I 
have indicated in the text, is Henry Statenโs [3mNietzscheโs Voice [0m(Cornell University Press, Ithaca, NY, 1990), a moving and 
profound series of meditations on some basic themes in Nietzsche. A less demanding and more critical work on an aspect of 
Nietzsche which has received little in the way of book-length attention is Julian Youngโs [3mNietzscheโs Philosophy of Art [0m(Cambridge 
University Press, Cambridge, 1992). Young finds a lot to be indignant about, but his criticisms, in their downrightness, are 
thought-provoking. A full-length book on [3mBT [0mby M. S. Silk and J. P. Stern is [3mNietzsche on Tragedy [0m(Cambridge University Press, 
Cambridge, 1981), which leaves no stone unturned, so far as the biographical background, the accuracy of Nietzscheโs account of 
Ancient Greece, and so on, are concerned. The essence of the work itself, and the source of its fascination, eludes them, but this 
is a mine of absorbing information. Nietzscheโs politics, or rather his seeming lack of them, are dealt with at length in two 
overlong but intermittently helpful books, both rather badly written. Tracy Strongโs [3mFriedrich Nietzsche and the Politics of 
Transfiguration [0m(expanded edn, University of California Press, Berkeley and Los Angeles, 1988) ranges very widely, and contains a 
particularly bizarre account of the Eternal Recurrence. Mark Warrenโs [3mNietzsche and Political Thought [0m(MIT Press, Cambridge, 
Mass., 1988) distinguishes between what Nietzscheโs political views, never presented systematically, were, and what they should 
have been, from the standpoint of the Frankfurt School of Critical Theory. 

There are many collections of essays by various commentators: one that has some excellent contributions to the reading of 
particular books is [3mReading Nietzsche [0m, edited by Robert C. Solomon and Kathleen M. Higgins (Oxford University Press, 1988). The 
way that Nietzsche tends to be read in France now is usefully illustrated in a book of translations of Derrida, Klossowski, 
Deleuze, and so on: [3mThe New Nietzsche, [0medited by David B. Allison (Delta, 1977). I find Gilles Deleuzeโs celebrated [3mNietzsche and 
Philosophy [0m(trans. Hugh Tomlinson, Athlone Press, London, 1983) quite wild about Nietzsche, but interesting about Deleuze. Many 
people swear by it. And we are in for an invasion of works from France, where Nietzsche has been idiosyncratically cultivated 
since World War II.
